   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d11783737" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The pursuit of high-capacity Na-ion batteries (NIBs) has propelled great forward the O3-type Ni-rich NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NCM) cathodes. However, the inborn chemo-mechanical instabilities caused by complex phase transitions and anisotropic lattice stress have severely delayed its widespread application. In the contribution, a cooperative surface-to-bulk modification strategy, i.e., in situ surface NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NTP) coating and bulk Ti/F co-doping, is first proposed to engineer an advanced NCM cathode (denoted as TF-NCM@NTP). The NTP protective layer with high thermodynamic stability and ionic conductivity endows TF-NCM@NTP with a highly stable interface and expedited de-/sodiated kinetics. The robust Ni/Co/Mn─F bond and concurrent Ti─O bond greatly strengthen the bulk lattice oxygen and mitigate the internal strain self-reproduction. Benefiting from these synergistic merits, the designed TF-NCM@NTP cathode demonstrates exceptional sodium-storage performance in terms of high-rate capacities and long-duration cycle life in both half and full cells. Particularly, the TF-NCM@NTP-based quasi-solid-state NIBs deliver an applicable material-level energy density of 285&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at 25&nbsp;°C, and exhibit wide-temperature-tolerant Na-storage behaviors ranging from −20 to 50&nbsp;°C. More essentially, the multi-level engineering strategy from surface to bulk developed here definitely makes enormous progress for rationally designing advanced cathode materials toward multi-scenario applicable NIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202412537-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202412537-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Na-ion batteries (NIBs) have recently attracted significant attention as promising candidates for large-scale electric energy storage (EES) devices due to the abundance and cost-effectiveness of sodium resources, along with their analogous operational mechanism to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0001_R_d11783729e393" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nano Energy">1</a>, <a href="#smll202412537-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0002_R_d11783729e393" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Sci.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Through extensive academic research, a wide range of functional materials have been proposed and utilized as cathode materials, exhibiting excellent electrochemical performance when combined with sodium metal as the counter electrode or employing the sodium compensation method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0003_R_d11783729e398" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">3</a>, <a href="#smll202412537-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0004_R_d11783729e398" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, practical application faces challenges such as inadequate initial Coulombic efficiency, insufficient reversible sodium content, and limited industrial feasibility arising from cathode materials that continue to hinder progress toward commercialization.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0005_R_d11783729e403" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a>-<a href="#smll202412537-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0007_R_d11783729e403" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Mater. Today">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Therefore, it is imperative to explore appropriate cathode materials that address these shortcomings in order to realize a practical Na-ion full-cell system. Among the various cathode materials, Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>TMO<sub>2</sub> compounds (transition metal (TM) = Ni, Co, Mn, Fe, Cr, etc.) have garnered significant attention as promising candidates due to their high theoretical specific capacity and straightforward synthesis process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0008_R_d11783729e416" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem.-Eur. J.">8</a>, <a href="#smll202412537-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0009_R_d11783729e416" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Int. J. Energy Res.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Depending on the coordination environment of Na<sup>+</sup> (forming octahedral or prismatic coordination with oxygen atoms) and the number of stacked sequences of oxygen atoms, the layered cathodes can be classified into P2, O3, P3, and O2 structures with particular interest focused on the former two oxides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0010_R_d11783729e424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Energy Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#smll202412537-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0011_R_d11783729e424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Storage Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The P2-type oxide cathode materials possess a broad planar tetrahedral center that serves as efficient Na<sup>+</sup> transport channels with low migration energy barriers, resulting in enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> conductivity and improved structural stability. However, their lower sodium content (<i>x</i> is approximately 2/3), as mentioned earlier also leads to reduced capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0012_R_d11783729e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">12</a>, <a href="#smll202412537-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0013_R_d11783729e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In comparison to the P2-type counterparts, the O3-type Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>TMO<sub>2</sub> exhibits a relatively higher specific capacity due to its relatively high sodium concentration (<i>x</i> &gt;&nbsp;0.8), although it requires overcoming a significant energy barrier for Na<sup>+</sup> migration.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0014_R_d11783729e451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">14</a>, <a href="#smll202412537-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0015_R_d11783729e451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Power Sources">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The O3-type Ni-rich NaNi<i><sub>x</sub></i>Co<i><sub>y</sub></i>Mn<sub>1−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i><sub>−</sub><i><sub>y</sub></i>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> ≥ 0.5) cathodes exhibit relatively high capacity but experience rapid capacity decay after prolonged cycling due to structural instability caused by multi-phase transitions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0016_R_d11783729e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Renew. Sust. Energy. Rev.">16</a>, <a href="#smll202412537-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0017_R_d11783729e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Storage Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Increasing the Ni content enhances the capacity of O3-type cathodes, yet it compromises their structural stability and cycling retention.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0018_R_d11783729e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Small">18</a>, <a href="#smll202412537-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0019_R_d11783729e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Power Sources">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, deep cycles induce intergranular cracks in O3-type Ni-rich cathodes as a result of continuous accumulation of lattice strain arising from abrupt lattice breathing. Moreover, flowable organic electrolyte easily infiltrate into the interior of particles through these cracks, leading to pulverization of original unabridged particles into the disconnected fragments.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0020_R_d11783729e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a>-<a href="#smll202412537-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0022_R_d11783729e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Angew. Chen. Int. Ed.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, irreversible phase transitions and pernicious side reactions with electrolyte on the particle surface obstruct Na<sup>+</sup> transfer channels and subsequently degrade performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0023_R_d11783729e492" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Ceram. Int.">23</a>, <a href="#smll202412537-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0024_R_d11783729e492" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - ACS Energy Lett.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To date, numerous strategies have been explored to enhance the stability of O3-type Ni-rich cathodes, including elemental doping, surface coating, and compositional distribution design. Cationic ion doping, such as Al<sup>3+</sup>, Ti<sup>4+</sup>and Sb<sup>5+</sup>, etc. offers a facile and effective approach for modifying the physical and/or chemical properties of cathode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0025_R_d11783729e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Phys. Chem. C">25</a>-<a href="#smll202412537-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0027_R_d11783729e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ACS Nano">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition to commonly used cations for element doping, anions like F<sup>−</sup> and Cl<sup>−</sup> have also been reported to improve structural stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0028_R_d11783729e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Eng. J.">28</a>, <a href="#smll202412537-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0029_R_d11783729e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Int. J. Electrochem. Sci.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Generally, introducing a surface buffer layer composed of inert metal oxides such as Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, MgO, and ZrO<sub>2</sub> is widely employed to mitigate interfacial chemical instabilities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0030_R_d11783729e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - T. Nonferr. Metals Soc.">30</a>-<a href="#smll202412537-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0032_R_d11783729e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Ceram. Int.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, besides oxide coatings mentioned above, polyanionic compounds like NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NTP) with a Na<sup>+</sup> fast ion conductor (NASICON) structure can be utilized as coating layers to enhance the cyclic and structural stability of cathode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0033_R_d11783729e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, due to inherent lattice strain within the material and inevitable surface side reactions, these aforementioned improvement strategies can only achieve partial success, particularly when employing single modification approaches.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0034_R_d11783729e547" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">34</a>, <a href="#smll202412537-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0035_R_d11783729e547" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Electrochim. Acta">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, there is an urgent need for a rational integration of these improvement strategies mentioned above into a multiple modification strategy that fully leverages their respective advantages.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, we purposefully developed a surface-to-bulk cooperative strategy that incorporates surface in situ NTP coating and bulk Ti/F (TF) co-doping to modify the O3-type Ni-rich NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NCM) cathode. In particular, an optimized formula of Na(Ni<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>)<sub>0.95</sub>Ti<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>1.99</sub>F<sub>0.01</sub>@NTP (denoted as TF-NCM@NTP) was accordingly constructed. Such a unique composition endows the remarkable sodium-storage performance due to several advantages it possesses. First, F<sup>−</sup> ion substitution decreased the covalency of the TM-O bond to relieve structural changes, meanwhile, F<sup>−</sup> doping stimulated O<sup>2−</sup> ion redox at high voltage (4.2&nbsp;V). Second, the ultrathin and uniform NTP with good thermal stability as an outmost buffer layer could effectively protect the bulk layered structure from electrolyte erosion and maintain the integrity of both structure and morphology. Third, the incorporation of high-valent Ti<sup>4+</sup> doping not only ensured the highly ordered crystalline structure but boosted the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion dynamics. Additionally, the Ti<sup>4+</sup> ions served as pillars and electron accumulators, reducing the degree of phase transition during cycling, and further suppressing the formation of intergranular cracks. As a competitive cathode for NIBs, our developed TF-NCM@NTP exhibited excellent chemo-mechanical stability and remarkable electrochemical Na-storage properties in both half-cells and full devices. Notably, when assembled into quasi-solid-state pouch-type full cells, TF-NCM@NTP demonstrated superior high-rate capabilities and long-term cycling stability over a wide temperature range from −20 to 50&nbsp;°C, which were attributed to the synergistic effect of our multi-level modification strategy proposed above.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202412537-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202412537-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202412537-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202412537-sec-0030-title">2.1 Physicochemical and Structural Characterizations</h3>
            
            <p>In this work, a cooperative surface-to-bulk modification strategy, as graphically displayed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202412537-fig-0001">1a</a>, is accomplished via a simple and feasible in situ coating approach and subsequent two-step low-temperature calcination. X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns are collected to obtain crystallographic structure information of the three samples (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0001">1b</a>). All characteristic peaks of the samples can be clearly assigned to the hexagonal layered <i>α</i>-NaFeO<sub>2</sub> structure, accompanied by the <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/68a5632f-a16d-443b-991d-b9a75e9d35a8/smll202412537-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202412537:smll202412537-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/smll202412537-math-0001.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <mi>R</mi>
                        <mover accent="true">
                           <mn>3</mn>
                           <mo>¯</mo>
                        </mover>
                        <mi>m</mi>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$R\bar 3m$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> space group (JCPDS no. 01-073-2048),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0036_R_d11783729e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Chem. Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> indicating that there is no discernible impact of Ti<sup>4+</sup>/F<sup>−</sup> and NTP coating on the bulk crystal structure of NCM cathodes. Moreover, an orderly arranged layered structure of the samples can also be confirmed by the distinct splitting observed in the (003) and (104) peaks (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0001">1c,d</a>). However, a noticeable shift toward a lower 2<i>θ</i> angle is observed in the characteristic peak (003), indicative of the expansion of the <i>c</i>-axis due to intercalation of additional ions into the lattice. Based on these findings, it can be unequivocally stated that our designed TF-NCM@NTP possesses a typical <i>α</i>-NaFeO<sub>2</sub> structure with the alternating distribution between Na-O and TM-O layers, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0001">1e</a>. To determine detailed crystal structures, we employ the Rietveld method to fit collected XRD data using GSAS/EXPGUI software. The Rietveld refinement results of NCM, F-NCM, and TF-NCM@NTP, as comparatively exhibited in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0001">1f–h</a>, demonstrate low <i>R</i><sub>p</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> factors that are consistent with both experimental and calculated data. These observations align well with the hexagonal <i>α</i>-NaFeO<sub>2</sub> structure with a space group of <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/3e01e716-1276-4e4c-a872-d9dafa895f53/smll202412537-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202412537:smll202412537-math-0002" display="inline" location="graphic/smll202412537-math-0002.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <mi>R</mi>
                        <mover accent="true">
                           <mn>3</mn>
                           <mo>¯</mo>
                        </mover>
                        <mi>m</mi>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$R\bar 3m$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>, further supported by the detailed structural analysis (Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1–S3</a>, Supporting Information). As enumerated, the refinement results indicate an enlargement in both lattice <i>a</i> and <i>c</i> parameters after modification, confirming the incorporation of Ti<sup>4+</sup> ions into the bulk crystal structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0037_R_d11783729e689" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Acta Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, compared to the Mn<sup>4+</sup> (0.53 Å), Ti<sup>4+</sup> ions, due to the larger ionic radius of 0.68 Å, can act as “pillars”, impeding slip between TM layers and enhancing the overall structural stability of NCM particles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0038_R_d11783729e699" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The estimated molar content of Ti in TF-NCM@NTP is estimated as ≈0.05, which does not disrupt the original layered structure at all and convincingly validates our practical modification strategy.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202412537-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c3bd80a1-692c-41ea-8f95-2388c261b8e3/smll202412537-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c3bd80a1-692c-41ea-8f95-2388c261b8e3/smll202412537-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/56648556-9901-4a94-bb74-852e325b11e2/smll202412537-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c3bd80a1-692c-41ea-8f95-2388c261b8e3/smll202412537-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202412537-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202412537" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration for the preparation process of TF-NCM@NTP. b) XRD patterns of NCM, F-NCM, and TF-NCM@NTP and partial magnification of peaks of c) (003) and d) (104). e) Crystallographic structure for the O3-type TF-NCM. Rietveld refinement results for f) NCM, g) F-NCM, and h) TF-NCM@NTP.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To gain deeper insights into the specific exterior/interfacial microstructures of the as-prepared samples and further verify the presence of NTP coating, a comparative analysis of field emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM) and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images is presented. All the three as-obtained samples show a uniform size distribution ranging between 6 – 8&nbsp;µm, with primary particles closely packed together (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202412537-fig-0002">2a–d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Even if after the in situ NTP coating, the TF-NCM@NTP (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0002">2c,d</a>) remains the smooth surface and the clearly visible square-shaped primary grains as compared to the single-phase NCM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0002">2a,b</a>). For detailed microstructure characterization, HRTEM images for a single TF-NCM@NTP particle are next employed to explicitly reveal the interfaces between TF-NCM and NTP (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0002">2e</a>). Obviously, a uniform, transparent, and continuous NTP coating film with a thickness of ≈5–10&nbsp;nm tightly adheres to the surface of TF-NCM cathode particles. Furthermore, two representative lattice fringes can be discerned from the boundaries of TF-NCM and NTP (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0002">2f,g</a>). As revealed, the bulk TF-NCM shows a typical layered structure, in which its (003) plane with an interplanar spacing of 5.26 Å extends continuously to the boundary, further confirmed by the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) analysis (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0002">2f</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0039_R_d11783729e751" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Power Sources">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As for the adjacent coating layer, a well-crystallized phase showing one set of lattice fringes with interplanar spacings of ≈2.67 Å, which highly coincides with the (018) planes of NTP rather than the bulk layered structure, is plainly distinct,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0040_R_d11783729e757" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Energy Storage">40</a>, <a href="#smll202412537-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0041_R_d11783729e757" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. CO2 Util.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as again demonstrated by the corresponding FFT images and intensity line profiles which is well related to the (018) planes. (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0002">2g</a>). To further investigate elemental composition and distribution on the surface of the cathode, energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) analysis is conducted on TF-NCM@NTP particles. As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0002">2h</a>, the results clearly demonstrate a homogeneous distribution of Na, Ni, Co, Mn, F, P, and Ti throughout the particle surface without any detectable presence of impurity elements.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202412537-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7a5e1db3-80bf-4370-8062-afe1be93f3a1/smll202412537-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/7a5e1db3-80bf-4370-8062-afe1be93f3a1/smll202412537-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/aebc75f9-acb3-4691-a95a-f77cbc42f300/smll202412537-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7a5e1db3-80bf-4370-8062-afe1be93f3a1/smll202412537-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202412537-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202412537" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">FESEM images of a,b) NCM and c,d) TF-NCM@NTP. e) HRTEM images for TF-NCM@NTP and f,g) corresponding zoomed-in view, SAED, FFT images, and intensity line profiles of relevant regions as indicated. h) TEM and matching EDS dot-mapping images of the single TF-NCM@NTP particle. i) XPS depth profiles of Ni, Co, Mn, F, P, and Ti with various etching depths of 0, 25, 50, 150, 300, and 450 s for TF-NCM@NTP.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To further acquire the subtle structural information and valence status of as-obtained TF-NCM@NTP, Ar-ion sputtering upon mounting time is implemented into the cathodes by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), which is an exclusive surface detection tool. A series of XPS profiles are obtained by progressively increasing etching depths at ≈0 (0 s), 5 (25 s), 10 (50 s), 30 (150 s), 60 (300 s), and 90 (450 s) nm for Ni, Co, Mn, F, P, and Ti elements. As clearly depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0002">2i</a>, due to the presence of an NTP coating layer on the surface, the bulk components including Ni, Co, and Mn, and the doping F ingredients are nearly undetectable at an initial etching depth of 0&nbsp;nm. However, with increasing etching depth beyond 5&nbsp;nm, there is a gradual rise in their content levels. Notably at an etching depth of ≈10&nbsp;nm onward, significant amounts of Ni, Co, Mn, and F elements can all be detected, which strongly evidences the existence of the uniform NTP coating layer with an average thickness ranging from 5 to 10&nbsp;nm. Upon further increasing the etching depth, a steady state in terms of both peak locations and intensities is reached for the Ni, Co, Mn, and F elements, indicating their uniform distribution. Expectedly, in contrast to this trend is the observed reversed distribution for both P and Ti elements. The maximum peak response is found on the surface of particles and gradually grows upon etching depth until it significantly decreases after passing through the coating layer. It is evident that the XPS spectrum of F demonstrates a notable shift toward higher binding energy. This phenomenon can be ascribed to the substitution of oxygen by F, resulting in the formation of a significantly stronger TM─F bond. Consequently, this leads to a reduction in the electron cloud density around F and a tighter binding of its inner electrons. Notably, even at an etching depth of 90&nbsp;nm, a trace amount of elemental Ti is still detectable due to its incorporation into the bulk phase during the sintering process. Consequently, there is no doubt that the NTP coating layer with good crystallization and highly stable structure is tightly bound to the outer surface, resulting in the formation of an unobstructed interface with excellent Na<sup>+</sup> permeability. Besides, the bulk lattice structure can be greatly consolidated because of the introduction of Ti and F doping components. Definitely, this design will exert a significant influence on enhancing the Na-storage behaviors of TF-NCM@NTP cathodes.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202412537-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202412537-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Evaluation</h3>
            
            <p>Electrochemical behaviors of all cathodes for Na-storage are initially evaluated in conventional coin-type half NIBs (referred to as H-NIBs) using metallic sodium as a counter electrode and a liquid ester-based electrolyte containing sodium perchlorate (NaClO<sub>4</sub>). In order to investigate the influence of doping and coating amounts on the electrochemical performance of NCM cathodes, tests on varying addition contents were conducted. The results demonstrate that the optimal electrochemical performance is achieved when the doping amount of F is 0.01 (molar ratio) and the coating amount of NTP is 0.05 (molar ratio) (Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202412537-fig-0003">3a</a>, TF-NCM@NTP exhibits an initial specific capacity of 148.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C (1&nbsp;C = 170&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>) within a voltage range of 2–4.3&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), which surpasses those of NCM (142.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and F-NCM (145.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), potentially resulting from the influence of Ti/F occupying active O<sup>2−</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0042_R_d11783729e834" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Appl. Phys. Lett.">42</a>, <a href="#smll202412537-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0043_R_d11783729e834" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Chem. Select">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, the initial Coulombic efficiencies (ICE) for NCM and F-NCM are determined as 73.3% and 80.2%, respectively, while TF-NCM@NTP yields an evidently higher ICE value of 94.2%. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0003">3b</a> illustrates rate behaviors for all three cathodes under different current densities (0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5&nbsp;C) within a potential range from 2 to 4.3&nbsp;V. It can be observed that both F-NCM and TF-NCM@NTP demonstrate higher first-cycle specific discharge capacities at low current rates (0.1&nbsp;C), as F doping stimulates O<sup>2−</sup> ion redox at high voltage (4.2&nbsp;V), thereby increasing specific capacity to some extent.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0044_R_d11783729e845" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - ACS Nano">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the TF-NCM@NTP presents remarkably improved rate capability, especially at high current rates. Of particular note, the TF-NCM@NTP renders a reversible specific capacity of 119.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5&nbsp;C, whereas NCM and F-NCM deliver specific capacities of 66.8 and 90.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, proving the enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> transfer dynamics through our developed modification strategy. The efficient Na-storage behaviors of TF-NCM@NTP are further evidenced in long-term cycling evaluation. As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0003">3c</a>, the TF-NCM@NTP can steady cycle even after 200 cycles with a capacity retention of 86.5%, which strikingly outperforms those of F-NCM (22.5%) and NCM (12.7%) in the same case, highlighting its significantly improved cycling stability, which should be attributed to the suppressed phase transition and structural degradation due to the presence of an NTP buffer layer combined with Ti/F co-doping. Particularly noteworthy is the exceptional cycling stability achieved here, to our knowledge, is comparable or much better than the state-of-the-art NIBs employing O3-type layered cathode materials (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, the voltage profiles corresponding to each cycle and derived differential capacity versus voltage (d<i>Q/</i>d<i>V</i>) curves are gathered throughout the 1st to 200th cycles at 1&nbsp;C within 2–4.3&nbsp;V. Notably, the TF-NCM@NTP manifests the minimal voltage separation upon cycling (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0003">3d</a>), accompanied by significantly reduced rightward shift values for both cathodic (0.12&nbsp;V) and anodic peaks (0.1&nbsp;V), as confirmed by matching d<i>Q/</i>d<i>V</i> plots (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0003">3e</a>). Moreover, even after 200 cycles, the d<i>Q/</i>d<i>V</i> responses remain sharp, strongly implying its exceptional electrochemical reversibility. Despite distinguishable voltage platforms and acceptable polarization gaps due to F introduction achieved by the F-NCM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0003">3f</a>), the relatively higher deviations in cathodic (0.19&nbsp;V) and anodic peak positions (0.13&nbsp;V) in comparison with TF-NCM@NTP can still be detected (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0003">3g</a>), highlighting the encouraging influence of Ti doping and/or NTP surface coating. In sharp comparison, the voltage platform of NCM rapidly diminishes and becomes indiscernible (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0003">3h</a>), along with noticeable shifts in both cathodic and anodic peaks by larger magnitudes of 0.21 and 0.18&nbsp;V, respectively, from their original positions (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0003">3i</a>). The enhanced cyclic ability demonstrated by our TF-NCM@NTP suggests that co-doping with F and Ti can greatly improve the lattice structure stability, and the NTP coating layer protects against the electrolyte side reactions while enhancing interfacial stability overall.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202412537-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9bd5bb14-627b-40ed-9026-a1fbea1a5032/smll202412537-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9bd5bb14-627b-40ed-9026-a1fbea1a5032/smll202412537-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/442979e1-7195-492b-9f0a-81d7d1711935/smll202412537-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9bd5bb14-627b-40ed-9026-a1fbea1a5032/smll202412537-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202412537-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202412537" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of half cells. a) Initial charge/discharge profiles (0.1&nbsp;C), b) rate behaviors, and c) cycling performance (1&nbsp;C) of NCM, F-NCM, and TF-NCM@NTP between 2 and 4.3&nbsp;V. Corresponding charge/discharge plots and derived d<i>Q/</i>d<i>V</i> curves for d,e) TF-NCM@NTP, f,g) F-NCM, and h,i) NCM.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The exceptional high-rate capability and cycling stability of TF-NCM@NTP can be ascribed to its significantly enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction dynamics and electron transfer coefficient. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves obtained from different cycling stages of the three cathodes (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202412537-fig-0004">4a–c</a>) demonstrate that the depolarization advantage is effectively manifested in TF-NCM@NTP (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0004">4a</a>), where all redox peaks remain prominent. In contrast, both the separation of redox potentials and peak current intensities for the other two cathodes, particularly NCM, undergo significant changes and nearly diminish under identical conditions, indicating sluggish sodiation/de-sodiation kinetics (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0004">4c</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0045_R_d11783729e937" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In particular, the redox peaks near 2.7/2.5&nbsp;V and 4.2/4.1&nbsp;V, corresponding to the O3-O3′/O3″-P3 phase transitions, exhibit more pronounced changes. Even after 200 cycles, TF-NCM@NTP maintains highly reversible redox reactions. In contrast, F-NCM struggles to retain the intensity of these redox peak pairs, while NCM performs even less satisfactorily. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) are further employed to shed light on the prominent promotion of our constructed hybrid structure. Generally, the semicircular feature in the high-frequency region corresponds to the charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) at electrode-electrolyte interfaces, whereas a linear tail exhibited in the low-frequency region correlates with Warburg impedance (<i>Z</i><sub>w</sub>), associated with Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0039_R_d11783729e951" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Power Sources">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Remarkably, varying degrees of enhancement are observed in <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> for all three cathodes during cycling (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0004">4d–f</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). Based on fitting results obtained from an equivalent circuit model (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), it is found that after 100 cycles, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values of NCM increase from 103.9 to 328.7 Ω, and further rise to 795.4 Ω after 200 cycles. Similarly, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values of F-NCM increase from 67.4 (the 10th) to 224.8 Ω (the 100th), and finally reach a value of 571.6 Ω (the 200th). Surprisingly, even after undergoing 200 cycles, TF-NCM@NTP demonstrates a significantly lower <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value of only 389.6 Ω. Furthermore, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) of the three cathodes in different states estimated from the GITT profiles after the 3rd (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0004">4g</a>) and 200th (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0004">4i</a>) cycles, respectively, at 1&nbsp;C within 2–4.3&nbsp;V are collected. Evidently, TF-NCM@NTP reveals extremely higher <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> values compared to both NCM and F-NCM, regardless of their initial state or after undergoing 200 cycles. Specifically, <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> values for TF-NCM@NTP are measured as 6.78 × 10<sup>−8</sup> and 8.77 × 10<sup>−9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which are considerably higher than those observed for F-NCM (5.25 × 10<sup>−8</sup>/6.24 × 10<sup>−10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>) and NCM (4.56 × 10<sup>−8</sup>/3.31 × 10<sup>−10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0004">4h,j</a>). Therefore, apart from the remarkable suppression of voltage decay over time, the superior electrochemical dynamics exhibited by TF-NCM@NTP are further evidenced by its lower <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> and higher <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> values. These examinations unequivocally validate the structural and compositional merits achieved by our TF-NCM@NTP cathode for efficient Na-storage performance.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202412537-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/23bcd806-dd84-4ec3-8c08-f8a611f894f2/smll202412537-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/23bcd806-dd84-4ec3-8c08-f8a611f894f2/smll202412537-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a2ded947-8591-4722-b9e9-8b0e274f363c/smll202412537-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/23bcd806-dd84-4ec3-8c08-f8a611f894f2/smll202412537-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202412537-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202412537" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Selected CV curves (0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>) of a) TF-NCM@NTP, b) F-NCM, and c) NCM as indicated. Nyquist plots of the three cathodes as indicated collected from the d) 10th, e) 100th, and f) 200th cycles. Typical GITT profiles for the charge/discharge process and corresponding calculated <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> at the g,h) 3rd and i,j) 200th cycles for the three cathodes as indicated.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The evolution of mechanical damage and structure degradation after cycling, which are closely related to the electrochemical behaviors, are inspected by FESEM, TEM, and geometric phase analyses (GPA). Apparently, the secondary particles of cycled NCM no matter after the 100th or 200th cycles suffered from serious breakdown, where plenty of microcracks take shape at the surface and extend all the way to the interior (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a–c</a>, Supporting Information). This will exacerbate the parasitic reactions due to the electrolyte penetration into the particle inside along the newly-formed microcracks, thereby leading to interface structure distortion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0046_R_d11783729e1069" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Eng. Fract. Mech.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In stark contrast, the well-retained secondary particle morphology and physical integrality are realized for TF-NCM@NTP after the identical cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5d–f</a>, Supporting Information), powerfully demonstrating the reinforced mechanical stability after our devised modification strategy. The more detailed morphology/structure evolutions upon cycling are further revealed by the cross-sectional SEM and TEM characterizations. Regarding the unmodified NCM, both the surface (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202412537-fig-0005">5a</a>) and cross-section (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0005">5b</a>) images show a significant number of intergranular cracks after 200 cycles, where the primary particles are totally pulverized into dispersed disconnected fragments, further confirmed by the evidence from TEM observations (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0005">5c</a>). The origin of intergranular crack formation can be attributed to the large accumulation of internal mechanical stress, as clearly observed from GPA analysis (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0005">5d</a>). Furthermore, the reproduction of these cracks combined with the concurrent formation of insulative NiO-like layers on particle surface undoubtedly leads to severe structural deterioration,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0047_R_d11783729e1093" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv. Energy Mater.">47</a>-<a href="#smll202412537-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0049_R_d11783729e1093" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> resulting in a substantial reduction in XRD peak intensities and expanded lattice spacing (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0005">5e</a>). Although the mechanical integrality is preserved after the 200th cycle for F-NCM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0005">5f</a>), some noticeable cracks inevitably develop thereafter (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0005">5g</a>). Such observation is further supported by the corresponding TEM image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0005">5h</a>) and GPA analysis (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0005">5i</a>), as well as the cycled XRD result, which shows a slight expansion in interplanar spacing and peak intensity fading (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0005">5j</a>). Thus, although the single F doping can strengthen the crystal structure to some extent, it cannot completely prevent crack formation. By contrast, no distinguishable cracks can be observed from the TF-NCM@NTP even undergoing 200 deep cycles with well-preserved morphology features (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0005">5k,l</a>). Noticeably, an ultrathin and uniform NTP coating layer with a thickness of ≈7&nbsp;nm is well-maintained on the cycled TF-NCM@NTP surface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0005">5m</a>), along with minimal stress concentration as compared to the other two samples even after 200 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0005">5n</a>). Moreover, the original O3-type layered structure remains nearly intact with a high degree of order and crystallinity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0005">5o</a>). Thus, the surface-to-bulk modification strategy we developed here can markedly reduce structure degradation-induced mechanical damages, thereby rendering exceptional cell performance.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202412537-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c3b9509c-3fb0-4171-a904-5e7213e6592d/smll202412537-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c3b9509c-3fb0-4171-a904-5e7213e6592d/smll202412537-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7c6d707c-dd75-4755-849e-71a9b587ceee/smll202412537-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c3b9509c-3fb0-4171-a904-5e7213e6592d/smll202412537-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202412537-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202412537" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Morphological and structural characterizations of the cycled a–e) NCM, f–j) F-NCM, and k–o) TF-NCM@NTP cathodes after 200th cycles (1&nbsp;C) between 2 and 4.3 V: a, f, k) particle-viewed and b,g,l) cross-sectional FESEM images, c,h,m) TEM images, d,i,n) GPA analysis and e,j,o) XRD patterns of NCM, f–j) F-NCM, and k–o) TF-NCM@NTP.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To unveil the dynamic structural evolution and phase transitions during (de)sodiation processes, electrochemical in situ XRD examinations measured at 0.1&nbsp;C within 2–4.3&nbsp;V during the first charge-discharge process are intentionally compared between the pristine NCM and TF-NCM@NTP. The full-scale in situ XRD results and corresponding contour plots of the partial zones, together with the matching discharge-charge plots for the NCM (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202412537-fig-0006">6a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a</a>, Supporting Information) and TF-NCM@NTP (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0006">6b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6b</a>, Supporting Information) uncover that both of them experience nearly identical reversible multi-step phase transformations. Nevertheless, it can be found that there is a noticeable distinction in terms of the deviation degree of the (003) peak (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0006">6c</a>) and expansion amplitude of <i>c</i>-axis parameters (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0006">6d</a>). At the beginning of charging, the characteristic signal corresponding to the original O3-type phase weakens and shifts toward a lower 2<i>θ</i> angle, indicating a gradual transformation from hexagonal O3 phase (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/7a913cf7-4ab0-4a5e-9388-ee5e9379f911/smll202412537-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202412537:smll202412537-math-0003" display="inline" location="graphic/smll202412537-math-0003.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <mi>R</mi>
                        <mover accent="true">
                           <mn>3</mn>
                           <mo>¯</mo>
                        </mover>
                        <mi>m</mi>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$R\bar 3m$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>) to monoclinic O'3 phase (C2&nbsp;m<sup>−1</sup>), which can be attributed to collective Jahn-Teller distortion induced by an increasing proportion of Ni<sup>3+</sup> during de-sodiation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0050_R_d11783729e1195" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Adv. Energy Mater.">50</a>-<a href="#smll202412537-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0052_R_d11783729e1195" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Small">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, this phase transition leads to simultaneous expansion along the <i>c</i>-axis lattice parameters. Subsequent de-sodiation causes the gradual disappearance of the O'3 phase and induces slip motion among TM layers, resulting in the formation of a new P3 hexagon phase as indicated by peak splitting at positions such as (003) and (104), along with shift observed for peaks like (101) and (102), toward higher 2<i>θ</i> angle. Upon deepening Na<sup>+</sup> removal (charged to 4.3&nbsp;V), the reduced Na<sup>+</sup> content within the main crystal structure induces a complete transition from the P3 to O″3 phase, accompanied by a continuous shift of the peak (003) toward higher 2<i>θ</i> angle and an intensified synchronous expansion of <i>c</i>-axis parameters. This accumulation of TM layers drives a multi-step complex phase transition as described above. Restoring the operating voltage from 4.3 to 2&nbsp;V enables a clear distinction of the sliding mechanism based on TM layers, indicating a highly reversible process with a complete opposite phase evolution from O'3 to P3, then back to O′3 before returning to its original O″3 phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0053_R_d11783729e1213" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Conversely, during sodium embedding process, there is gradual shrinkage in <i>c</i>-axis lattice parameters after reaching their maximum value during sodium removal stage. The repeated expansion and contraction of <i>c</i>-axis lattice parameters during sodium conversion leads to the accumulation of local internal strain, severely compromising mechanical and structural stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0054_R_d11783729e1223" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv. Mater.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The variation range of the (003) diffraction peak and the evolution of <i>c</i>-axis lattice parameters corresponding to TF-NCM@NTP and NCM during charge and discharge are depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0006">6c,d</a>. Although the variation trends of both materials are consistent, meticulous observation reveals a slight deviation of only 0.07&nbsp;° in the diffraction peaks of TF-NCM@NTP (003) during the charging process, which is noticeably smaller than the 0.1&nbsp;° observed for NCM under identical conditions (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0006">6c</a>). Similar changes can also be observed in the <i>c</i>-axis lattice parameters. Notably, the change value (Δ) of the <i>c</i>-axis lattice parameters for TF-NCM@NTP measures at 0.05 Å, exhibiting a markedly reduction of 0.06 Å compared to NCM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0006">6d</a>). This recurrent fluctuation in diffraction peak position and <i>c</i>-axis lattice parameters during charge-discharge process may induce slip within TM layers, thereby weakening Na-TM repulsion forces and resulting in capacity attenuation and structural degradation. The examinations here undeniably corroborate the robust structural affordability of our designed TF-NCM@NTP cathode for efficient Na-storage.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202412537-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4283a774-330a-466f-af1d-5581c19fc9d3/smll202412537-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4283a774-330a-466f-af1d-5581c19fc9d3/smll202412537-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/038528bb-4412-4fc0-a7d3-fc5877b7b894/smll202412537-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4283a774-330a-466f-af1d-5581c19fc9d3/smll202412537-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202412537-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202412537" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">In situ XRD contour plots and corresponding potential-time plots during the initial charge/discharge cycle for a) NCM and b) TF-NCM@NTP at 0.1&nbsp;C within 2–4.3&nbsp;V. c) Evolution of (003) peak, and d) variations of fitted <i>c</i>-axis lattice parameters overcharging and discharging processes for NCM and TF-NCM@NTP.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To further evaluate the performance of the TF-NCM@NTP cathode in practical applications, coin-type full cells paired with hard carbon (HC) as anode are fabricated. The selected HC anode provides a reversible specific capacity of ≈308.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with an ICE of ≈82% and a capacity retention of 74.6% after 100 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). As displayed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202412537-fig-0007">7a</a>, the TF-NCM@NTP||HC full NIBs (F-NIBs) can yield an initial discharge capacity of 147.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with an ICE as high as ≈90.4% within 2–4.3&nbsp;V, comparable to the values obtained in the half cells. Even at a higher rate of 5 C, as illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information), after 200 cycles, F-NIBs exhibit commendable cycle stability, maintaining a capacity retention of 78.8%. Besides, the F-NIBs show an outstanding rate capability, maintaining a competitive capacity of 96.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> when the current density increases to 5&nbsp;C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0007">7b</a>). Moreover, F-NIBs also exhibit exceptional long-term cycling stability with a remarkable capacity retention rate as high as 86.7% after undergoing 200 cycles at 1&nbsp;C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0007">7c</a>). Thus, it can be preliminarily concluded that our designed TF-NCM@NTP cathode demonstrates excellent feasibility for practical Na-storage applications. The efficient Na-storage behaviors of F-NIBs have motivated us to further investigate its feasibility in pouch-type quasi-solid-state full NIBs (QS-FNIBs) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information), which employs the TF-NCM@NTP as cathode and HC as anode and polyvinylidene fluoride hexafluoropropylene-based gel previously developed by us as the quasi-solid electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0050_R_d11783729e1300" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Adv. Energy Mater.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The radar plots, as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0007">7d</a>, strongly demonstrate the significant advantages of TF-NCM@NTP-based QS-FNIBs in terms of energy density, ICE, rate behaviors, average voltage, and cycling stability. Encouragingly, based on the total mass of both cathode and anode components, the TF-NCM@NTP-based QS-FNIBs can achieve an impressive energy density up to 285&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at 25&nbsp;°C, surpassing most recently reported full NIBs (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). The QS-FNIBs in normal form afford an average operating voltage of ≈2.89&nbsp;V and can easily power a “heart-shaped” string of small LED lights (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0007">7e</a>). Attractively, our QS-FNIBs are able to keep these “heart-shaped” lights working without any fires or explosions even when subjected to abusive nail penetration and cutting deformation into two parts (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0007">7e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), strongly featuring their extremely reliable security properties.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202412537-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/92a6cee8-5ac0-450e-8376-f10d46b629bc/smll202412537-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/92a6cee8-5ac0-450e-8376-f10d46b629bc/smll202412537-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1e567231-a4ea-4e19-a4fa-5d82837f7b45/smll202412537-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/92a6cee8-5ac0-450e-8376-f10d46b629bc/smll202412537-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202412537-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202412537" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical evaluation of F-NIBs and QS-FNIBs. a) Typical voltage-capacity profiles of NCM@NTP and HC electrodes, b) rate behaviors, and c) cycling stability of F-NIBs within 2–4.3&nbsp;V. d) Radar plot summarizing the properties of NCM and TF-NCM@NTP in QS-FNIBs. e) Digital images for thermal security assessment under the nail penetration and corner-cut tests. f) Initial charge/discharge plots, g) rate behaviors, h) long-term cycling performance, and i–m) corresponding charge/discharge curves upon cycling of QS-FNIBs at various temperatures as indicated.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To further investigate the practicality of our QS-FNIBs, we explore the tolerance of their electrochemical properties to ambient temperatures ranging from −20 to 50&nbsp;°C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0055_R_d11783729e1345" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Appl. Phys. Rev.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0007">7f</a> illustrates the initial charge-discharge plots of QS-FNIBs at a rate of 0.1&nbsp;C within 2–4.3&nbsp;V under different temperatures. Evidently, the QS-FNIBs can achieve an initial discharge capacity and ICE of 148.1 (88.1%) and 152.4 (90.2%) mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 25 and 50&nbsp;°C, respectively. Even under rigorous temperatures of 0 and −10&nbsp;°C, the competitive discharge capacities of up to 141.2 (93.7%) and 134.7 (91.6%) mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can be readily obtained, respectively. As for the more demanding scenarios of −20&nbsp;°C, an acceptable discharge capacity of 120.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with an ICE value as high as 88.4% can still be endowed. Besides achieving high reversible capacities at low current rates, excellent high-rate capabilities are enabled in our QS-FNIBs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0007">7g</a>). Obviously, the QS-FNIBs reveal large discharge capacities of 100.4 and 89.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 and 5&nbsp;C, respectively, for the case of 25&nbsp;°C. Moreover, the competitive discharge capacities achieved at temperatures of 50, 0, and −10&nbsp;°C are 79.3, 80.0, and 51.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> under a high rate of 5&nbsp;C, respectively. Even at ultra-low temperatures of −20&nbsp;°C, the device still exhibits an acceptable discharge capacity of 35.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5&nbsp;C, demonstrating its exceptional high-rate capabilities. The nearly temperature-independent Na-storage behaviors are further confirmed by long-term cycle stability tests. After an initial three-turn activation cycle at a low C rate (0.1&nbsp;C), prolonged cycling is performed up to 200 cycles at 1&nbsp;C rate within 2–4.3&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0007">7h</a>). Remarkably, the QS-FNIBs well retains a large discharge capacity of 111.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles at 25&nbsp;°C with a capacity retention of 91.8%, which means the capacity attenuation per cycle is only 0.041%. More importantly, we also evaluate the electrochemical cycling stability of QS-FNIBs under high-rate conditions (5 C, 25&nbsp;°C), as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information). Even after 200 cycles under these demanding conditions, the QS-FNIBs exhibit impressive capacity retention of 83.1% (decreasing from 100.6 to 83.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). As the temperature further increases to 50&nbsp;°C, our QS-FNIBs likewise exhibit remarkable capacity retention undergoing the same cycles (from 124.4 to 85.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), with an average attenuation of only 0.154% per cycle. As for the case of 0&nbsp;°C, the capacity decay is perfectly acceptable, and the capacity retention reaches 88.7% (from 121.3 to 107.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). Furthermore, the QS-FNIBs show satisfactory capacity retention of 77.1% even at −10&nbsp;°C (from 110.9 to 85.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). More encouragingly, under extremely harsh temperatures as low as −20&nbsp;°C, an impressive capacity retention of 79.6% (from 97.8 to 77.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) is still achieved. The charge/discharge curves matching with each temperature upon cycling are provided to confirm the aforementioned findings (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0007">7i–m</a>). Clearly, the average voltage platforms for the case of 25&nbsp;°C can be well preserved even when suffering from 200 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0007">7i</a>). As for other temperatures, despite a slight loss in discharge capacity during the initial few cycles, subsequent charge-discharge processes showcase excellent repeatability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202412537-fig-0007">7j–m</a>), strongly confirming the significantly reduced electrochemical polarization. All the results above undisputedly validate the temperature-independence-level Na-storage capability of our QS-FNIBs toward practical applications.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202412537-sec-0050">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202412537-sec-0050-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, the chemo-mechanical instabilities including the structure/interface degradation and mechanical breakdowns confronting the O3-type Ni-rich cathodes for NIBs were considerably ameliorated by our proposed cooperative modification strategy, which rationally combined in situ nano NTP surface coating and bulk Ti/F co-doping. First, the in situ formation of NTP protective layers with high Na<sup>+</sup> conductive capability rendered a sturdy and unimpeded cathode/electrolyte interphase, thereby effectively prohibiting the fatal surface parasitic reactions and interface structure degeneration. Second, bulk F doping promoted the O<sup>2−</sup> redox reaction to enable an improved initial discharge capacity, and the simultaneously incorporated stronger Ti─O covalence bond guaranteed a robust crystal framework and lattice oxygen because of the rearrangement of cations within the TM-layer. Furthermore, the introduced Ti<sup>4+</sup> within the TM─O layers played a “pillar effect” role, further strengthening the structure tolerance of TF-NCM@NTP. Due to the synergistic effect of structural and component advantages, the TF-NCM@NTP exhibited outstanding cycling stability and rate behaviors in both coin-type and pouch-type full cells. Especially, the TF-NCM@NTP-based QS-FNIBs were endowed with exceptional Na-storage properties over a wide temperature ranging from −20 to 50&nbsp;°C, showing commendable capacity retention of 91.8% (25&nbsp;°C) and 79.6% (−20&nbsp;°C) after 200 deep cycles, and high-rate capabilities (89.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 and 25&nbsp;°C), along with a competitive energy density of 285&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at 25&nbsp;°C. Furthermore, the TF-NCM@NTP-based QS-FNIBs revealed greatly enhanced safety capabilities, which survived under challenging nail penetration and corner-cut tests without any thermal-driven dangers. Our findings here provided valuable insights for addressing the challenges arising from structural and interfacial instability in other layered cathode materials for next-generation NIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202412537-sec-0060">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202412537-sec-0060-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202412537-sec-0070">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202412537-sec-0080">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202412537-sec-0090">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202412537-sec-0090-title"> Synthesis of NCM, F-NCM, TF-NCM@NTP</h4>
                  
                  <p>The spherical hydroxide precursors Ni<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>(OH)<sub>2</sub> were obtained according to our optimized coprecipitation method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202412537-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202412537-bib-0056_R_d11783729e1444" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Sci. Adv.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To synthesize NCM, the as-prepared Ni<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>(OH)<sub>2</sub> precursors were evenly mixed with NaOH in a glove box for 1&nbsp;h, and the molar ratio was adjusted to 1:1.05 to make up for volatilization of NaOH at high temperatures. The calcination process was carried out in a tubular furnace full of O<sub>2</sub> atmosphere, and the crystallized temperature and time were set as 720&nbsp;°C and 24&nbsp;h, respectively. To obtain F-doped NCM (F-NCM), the synthetic procedures and parameters were identical to those of NCM, just with the presence of NaF in a certain molar ratio. The as-obtained F-NCM was fully ground with a certain proportion of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, nano-sized TiO<sub>2</sub>, and NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> in the glove box, and then calcinated in a tubular furnace filled with Ar at 670&nbsp;°C for 6&nbsp;h, then TF-NCM@NTP was finally achieved.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202412537-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202412537-sec-0100-title"> Characterization of Materials</h4>
                  
                  <p>The samples were characterized by X-ray diffractometer (XRD) at 2° min<sup>−1</sup> on Rigaku Ultima IV (Cu K<i>α</i> radiation, λ = 1.5046&nbsp;Å, 40&nbsp;KV, 120&nbsp;mA, Japan). The detailed data of crystal structure were analyzed by the Rietveld program GSAS/EXPGUI software. The morphologies of the samples were detected by 15&nbsp;kV FESEM (JEOL6300 F). On Thermo ESCALAB 250Xi, 284.8&nbsp;eV (C 1s) energy calibration, Ar ion etching at a rate of 0.2&nbsp;nm s<sup>−1</sup>, and XPS were used to obtain the element species and depth-dependent valence information of materials. Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) (JEOL JEM 2100 system, operating voltage 200&nbsp;kV) were used to characterize the microstructure. In situ, XRD data for the first cycle were collected at 15° min<sup>−1</sup> within 10–80°. For postmortem characterization, after washed with dimethyl carbonate (DMC), the re-cycled cathodes after various cycles were collected from the disassembled cells and dried in a glove box filled with Ar.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202412537-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202412537-sec-0110-title"> Electrochemical Measurement</h4>
                  
                  <p>The prepared electroactive samples (NCM, F-NCM, or TF-NCM@NTP), carbon black, and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) (mass ratio 8:1:1) were dispersed on Al foil by N-methyl-2 pyrrolidone (NMP) and dried in a vacuum drying oven at 120&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h to prepare the working positive electrodes. The mass loading of active material on each electrode was about 7.5 ±&nbsp;0.15&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. For H-NIBs, the metal Na-foil was selected as the anode, and the liquid electrolyte employed was 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in a mixed solvent containing ethylene carbonate (EC) and dimethyl carbonate (DMC) solvents (1:1 in volume) with 5&nbsp;wt.% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC), and commercial glass microfiber diaphragms were used as separators. For F-NIBs and QS-FNIBs, an NMP dispersion mixture composed of commercial HC (BTR New Materials Group Limited), carbon black, and PVDF (7:2:1 in weight) was used as the anode. Note that, the anodes for F-NIBs and QS-FNIBs were coated on Cu foil, placed in a 60&nbsp;°C oven overnight, and cut to the appropriate size as the anode, and the capacity ratio of anode to cathode was designed to be ≈1.1–1.2:1. The electrolytes employed in both F-NIBs and QS-FNIBs were identical with that of the H-NIBs. All the cells were assembled in a glove box filled with Ar (MBRAUN UNIlabs Plus, O<sub>2</sub>, and H<sub>2</sub>O contents were all less than 0.1&nbsp;ppm). Electrochemical evaluation at different temperatures was carried out on a LAND test system (CT2001A, Wuhan Jinuo Electronic Co., Ltd., China) in a temperature-controllable test chamber (Dongguan Kingjio TT5166 Tempe and humid control equipment Co., Ltd., China). The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was performed on the LAND test system (CT2001A, Wuhan Jinuo Electronic Co., Ltd., China). EIS was tested on a CHI600E electrochemical workstation at 4.3&nbsp;V, 0.01–100000&nbsp;Hz, and an amplitude of 10&nbsp;mV.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
